<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41598"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Draft genome sequence of adzuki bean, Vigna angularis"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Reports 2015 5:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2015-01-28"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2015 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Adzuki bean (Vigna angularis var. angularis) is a dietary legume crop in East Asia. The presumed progenitor (Vigna angularis var. nipponensis) is widely found in East Asia, suggesting speciation and domestication in these temperate climate regions. Here, we report a draft genome sequence of adzuki bean. The genome assembly covers 75% of the estimated genome and was mapped to 11 pseudo-chromosomes. Gene prediction revealed 26,857 high confidence protein-coding genes evidenced by RNAseq of different tissues. Comparative gene expression analysis with V. radiata showed that the tissue specificity of orthologous genes was highly conserved. Additional re-sequencing of wild adzuki bean, V. angularis var. nipponensis and V. nepalensis, was performed to analyze the variations between cultivated and wild adzuki bean. The determined divergence time of adzuki bean and the wild species predated archaeology-based domestication time. The present genome assembly will accelerate the genomics-assisted breeding of adzuki bean."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Adzuki bean (<i>Vigna angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i>) is a dietary legume crop in East Asia. The presumed progenitor (<i>Vigna angularis</i> var. <i>nipponensis</i>) is widely found in East Asia, suggesting speciation and domestication in these temperate climate regions. Here, we report a draft genome sequence of adzuki bean. The genome assembly covers 75% of the estimated genome and was mapped to 11 pseudo-chromosomes. Gene prediction revealed 26,857 high confidence protein-coding genes evidenced by RNAseq of different tissues. Comparative gene expression analysis with <i>V. radiata</i> showed that the tissue specificity of orthologous genes was highly conserved. Additional re-sequencing of wild adzuki bean, <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>nipponensis</i> and <i>V. nepalensis</i>, was performed to analyze the variations between cultivated and wild adzuki bean. The determined divergence time of adzuki bean and the wild species predated archaeology-based domestication time. The present genome assembly will accelerate the genomics-assisted breeding of adzuki bean.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Adzuki bean (<i>Vigna angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i>) is a diploid legume crop (2n = 2x = 22) with an estimated genome size of 538 mega bases (Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Parida, A., Raina, S. N. &amp; Narayan, R. K. J. Quantitative DNA Variation between and within Chromosome Complements of Vigna Species (Fabaceae). Genetica 82, 125–133 (1990)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d38204e775">1</a></sup>. It is one of the Asian <i>Vigna</i> in the Ceratotropis subgenus, under the papilionoid subfamily of the Fabaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Tomooka, N., Vaughan, D. &amp; Moss, H. The Asian Vigna: genus Vigna subgenus Ceratotropis genetic resources. (Kluwer, Dordrecht, 2002)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d38204e782">2</a></sup>. Adzuki bean is widely cultivated in East Asian countries like China, Japan and Korea as an ingredient for traditional dessert cuisines due to its sweet taste, as well as its nutritious protein and starch contents. The annual cultivation area for adzuki bean in China, Japan, Korean peninsula and Taiwan is estimated to be 670,000, 120,000, 30,000 and 20,000 ha, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Rubatzky, V. E., Yamaguchi, M., Rubatzky, V. &amp; Yamaguchi, M. World vegetables: principles, production and nutritive values. (Chapman &amp; Hall New York, 1997)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d38204e787">3</a></sup>. The wild species of adzuki bean such as <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>nipponensis</i>, <i>V. nakashimae</i> and <i>V. nepalensis</i>, are widely distributed across East Asia and Himalayan countries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Tomooka, N., Vaughan, D. &amp; Moss, H. The Asian Vigna: genus Vigna subgenus Ceratotropis genetic resources. (Kluwer, Dordrecht, 2002)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d38204e803">2</a></sup>. However, archaeological evidences suggested multiple domestication origins in northeast Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lee, G.-A. Archaeological perspectives on the origins of azuki (Vigna angularis). Holocene 23, 453–459 (2013)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d38204e808">4</a></sup>.</p><p>Several important legume crops and model plants have been sequenced. This includes warm-season legumes such as <i>Glycine max</i>, <i>Phaseolus vulgaris</i>, <i>Cajanus cajan</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d38204e823">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat Biotechnol 30, 83–U128 (2012)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d38204e826">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Schmutz, J. et al. A reference genome for common bean and genome-wide analysis of dual domestications. Nat Genet 46, 707–713 (2014)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d38204e829">7</a></sup> and <i>Vigna radiata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Kang, Y. J. et al. Genome sequence of mungbean and insights into evolution within Vigna species. Nat Commun 5, 5443 (2014)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d38204e835">8</a></sup>. <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> is a close relative of <i>V. radiata</i> and is adapted to sub-tropical and temperate climate zone. In spite of its economic importance and the demands for improved <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> variety, genomic studies for agriculturally important traits and efficient breeding methods for this species have been lacking. Elucidation of the genome sequence of <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> could reveal the general genome structure and evolution of this legume species in comparison to closely related genomes and greatly assist comparative genomics of <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> and other well-studied legume genomes. In addition, the re-sequencing efforts of cultivated and wild adzuki beans will facilitate the measurement of genetic diversity of each locus and the development of useful markers for putative domestication-related loci.</p><p>Here, we assembled a draft genome of adzuki bean into pseudo-chromosomes using sequence data from next generation sequencing. This adzuki bean genome was compared to other warm-season legumes to study genome evolution and speciation. Well established quantitative trait loci (QTLs) of <i>G. max</i> were translated into the adzuki bean genome using gene order conservation. In addition, we sequenced two wild adzuki bean species, <i>Vigna angularis</i> var. <i>nipponensis</i> and <i>Vigna</i> <i>nepalensis</i> and identified the putative loci related to domestication in order to develop SNP markers for more efficient breeding. Our genome sequence of <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> and QTL-associated genetic markers will boost genomics of warm season legumes and breeding programs of adzuki bean.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly</h3><p><i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> has a diploid genome. Based on flow cytometry analysis the genome size was estimated to be 612 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S1</a>) which is higher than the previous estimate of 538 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Parida, A., Raina, S. N. &amp; Narayan, R. K. J. Quantitative DNA Variation between and within Chromosome Complements of Vigna Species (Fabaceae). Genetica 82, 125–133 (1990)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d38204e913">1</a></sup>. The selected domesticated line for sequencing was Gyeongwon, a widely grown variety in Korea, which was developed by the Rural Development Administration (RDA) in Korea to reduce root lodging and to improve grain quality.</p><p>For <i>de novo</i> genome assembly, we prepared two paired-end libraries with 180 bp insert size, along with two 5 kb mate pair and one 10 kb mate-pair libraries for 100 bp short read sequencing using the Illumina HiSeq 2000 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S2</a>). A single linear library was also constructed for sequencing using the Roche GS-FLX+ producing total 1,288,628 reads with average read length, 458 bp. Approximately 291-fold sequence read coverage of the estimated genome size was generated by the two sequencing platforms. For reads generated by the Illumina HiSeq 2000, ALLPATHS-LG assembler was used for <i>de novo</i> assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A 108, 1513–1518 (2011)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d38204e929">9</a></sup>. The long reads generated by GS FLX+ were assembled using Newbler software and the resulting contigs were transformed into paired end reads with 180 bp insert and used as input for ALLPATHS-LG assembly. Using Jellyfish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Marcais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764–770 (2011)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d38204e933">10</a></sup> software at 22 k-mer frequency, we estimated the genome size to be 591 Mb which is close to the size calculated using flow cytometry analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S1</a>). The assembly produced 3,883 scaffolds with proper read coverage statistics of sequencing libraries including the pseudo-library from Newbler assembly and the N50 length of the scaffolds was 703 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S3</a>). The sum of the scaffold length was approximately 443 Mb covering 75 percent of the estimated genome size (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S4</a>).</p><p>To link the scaffolds into super-scaffolds, we utilized the synteny relationship between <i>V. angularis</i> and closely related legume genomes such as <i>Phaseolus vulgaris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Schmutz, J. et al. A reference genome for common bean and genome-wide analysis of dual domestications. Nat Genet 46, 707–713 (2014)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d38204e955">7</a></sup> and <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i>. This was based on the assumption that the gene order among closely-related warm season legume species is highly conserved. Identification of the synteny relationship and calculation of the Ks values for each orthologous or paralogous gene pair was performed using MCSCANX software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, Y. P. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d38204e965">11</a></sup>. We retrieved the synteny blocks from the most recent peak in the Ks frequency plot (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S2</a>) and extracted conserved genomic blocks that showed multi-species collinearity among the three legume genomes to be used as bridges for super-scaffolding (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S3</a>). The synteny-based scaffolding strategy improved the N50 length from 704 kb to 1.5 Mb and the maximum length of scaffolds from 4.4 Mb to 11.1 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S5</a>).</p><p>To assemble the pseudo-molecules, we implemented the genotyping by sequencing (GBS) method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Elshire, R. J. et al. A robust, simple genotyping-by-sequencing (GBS) approach for high diversity species. Plos One 6, e19379 (2011)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d38204e982">12</a></sup> to construct a high-density genetic map of adzuki bean. The mapping population comprised of 133 F<sub>4</sub> lines generated by single-seed descent from a cross between <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> (Gyeongwon) and the wild species <i>V. nakashimae</i> (IT178530). GBS short reads were mapped to genomic regions flanked by ApeKI restriction sites using Bowtie2 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat Methods 9, 357–359 (2012)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d38204e998">13</a></sup>. A total of 4,524 segregating SNP sites were identified. Possible co-segregating sites within 1 kb regions were merged reducing the total SNP number to 2,347. However, more than half of the SNPs showed segregation distortions that are probably due to interspecific crossing (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S6</a>). Removal of the distorted SNPs resulted in a high-density genetic map with 814 SNPs in 11 linkage groups (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S7</a>). In total, 158 scaffolds could be anchored to the genetic map to construct the 11 pseudo-chromosomes. The sum of the anchored scaffolds was 210 Mb and the length of N50 was 25 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S8</a>). However, 78 scaffolds and super-scaffolds were anchored without orientation information, because only a single marker could be used to anchor them to the map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S4</a>). Total 45 super-scaffolds were anchored to genetic map and 43 super-scaffolds were consistent with our genetic map suggesting the reliability of the synteny-based scaffolding method (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S9</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Prediction of genes and repetitive sequences in adzuki bean</h3><p>After masking the identified repetitive sequences, we implemented the structural and homology-based gene prediction procedure according to the MAKER pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr Protoc Bioinformatics 25, 4.10.1–4.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1032">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: An easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1035">15</a></sup>. In order to obtain direct evidence of gene expression, we extracted mRNA from adzuki bean flower, pod, leaf and root tissues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S10</a>). The mRNA samples were sequenced using the Illumina HiSeq 2000 for subsequent <i>de novo</i> assembly using Trinity software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat Biotechnol 29, 644–U130 (2011)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1045">16</a></sup>. The assembled contigs were pooled and supplied to the MAKER pipeline as the evidence of transcription and 26,857 high-confident genes were predicted. Using CEGMA pipeline, more than 86 percent of 248 core eukaryotic genes (CEG) could be completely matched to our genome assembly and the 99 percent of 248 CEGs matched to the predicted proteins using BLASTP algorithm with <i>E</i>-value 1e-10 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S4</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genornes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1056">17</a></sup>. To test the reliability of the predicted <i>V. angularis</i> gene set, we compared the sequence length distributions of the genes, coding DNA sequences (CDS) and introns to the gene models of <i>P. vulgaris</i>, <i>G. max</i> and <i>V. radiata.</i> The density plot showed consistent distributions of CDS and intron length among the three legume genomes. However, the proportion of short genes (~250 bp) was higher in <i>V. angularis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S5</a>). Of the 26,857 high-confident genes, 15,976 were located on pseudo chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#Fig1">Fig. 1a, 1b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S4</a>). Clustering analysis was performed on the protein sets of <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> and the protein sequences of <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>O. sativa,</i> and <i>G. max</i> using OrthoMCL software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: Identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1104">18</a></sup> and identified 6,643 gene clusters that are shared among all five species and 1,163 clusters that are specific to <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S11</a>). We could assign functional annotations to 21,532 genes using InterProScan and BLAST against Arabidopsis proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep08069/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep08069/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep08069_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep08069/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep08069_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="447" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Summary of genome assembly of cultivated adzuki bean and analyses of genes, repetitive sequences and predicted QTLs.</p><p>(A) Circular map showing predicted QTL regions of <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> based on the synteny relationship with <i>G. max</i> and the WGD regions displayed by the grey ribbons at most inner part of circle. From outer to inner layer, four types of QTLs are depicted (disease resistance, flowering time, pod maturity and seed weight, respectively). Highlighted region of each layers represent the candidate physical position of the related QTLs. The red dots on the chromosomes represent the loci of NBS-LRR domain containing genes. (B) The distribution of the predicted genes and the repetitive sequences in adzuki bean genome plotted by 300 kb bin length. The colored bars represent the counts of genes and repeated elements in each bin; LTR/Gypsy (green), LTR/Copia (blue), LINE (blue) and genes (red). (C) Venn diagram depicting the clustering analysis of the five protein sets from <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>G. max</i>, <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>V. angularis</i>. The numbers of homolog clusters were indicated for each species and species intersection.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep08069/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The predicted gene content of <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> showed extensive synteny relationship with closely-related warm season legumes including <i>G. max</i>, <i>P. vulgaris</i> and <i>V. radiata</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#Fig2">Fig. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S6</a>). We examined the tissue specificity of gene expressions in <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> and <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> using RNA-Seq data from four different tissues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S7</a>). There are 9,196 orthologs between <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> and <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> that showed persistent tissue specificity, suggesting that gene functions were extensively retained even after speciation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S12</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep08069/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep08069/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep08069_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep08069/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep08069_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="332" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Visualization of the synteny relationships among the closely related warm season legumes, <i>V. angularis</i>, <i>V. radiata</i>, <i>P. vulgaris</i> and <i>G. max</i>.</p><p>The x-axis indicates chromosomal locations of genes in synteny relationship and the y-axis indicates Ks value of the corresponding gene pair, showing both conservation of gene order as well as chromosomal rearrangements of synteny blocks.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep08069/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Using the Pfam annotations of each protein, we classified transcription factors according to the rules described in Lang et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Lang, D. et al. Genome-Wide Phylogenetic Comparative Analysis of Plant Transcriptional Regulation: A Timeline of Loss, Gain, Expansion and Correlation with Complexity. Genome Biol Evol 2, 488–503 (2010)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1264">19</a></sup>. In total, 2,669 genes encoding transcription factors (TFs) were identified in the adzuki bean genome. We compared the relative TF abundance with that of other plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S13</a>) and found the overall proportions of TF gene families to be similar in these plant genomes. However, bZIP2 consistently makes up less than 1% of the total TFs in legume genomes. This contrasts with non-legume genomes such as <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>Z. mays</i>, <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>B. distachyon</i> where bZIP2 represented more than 3% of all TFs, suggesting a possible gene loss event of this TF family in the common ancestor of legume plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S13</a>).</p><p>We surveyed the repetitive sequences within the scaffold sequences to examine the abundance and distribution of transposable elements, which are known as major drivers of genome evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Lisch, D. How important are transposons for plant evolution? Nat Rev Genet 14, 49–61 (2013)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1290">20</a></sup>. Homology- and structure-based analysis revealed approximately 43.1% of sequenced adzuki bean genome as repetitive sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S14</a>). As in the other legumes and in that of the closely related <i>V. radiata</i>, the predominant repetitive sequences were annotated as long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1300">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat Biotechnol 30, 83–U128 (2012)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1303">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of chickpea (Cicer arietinum) provides a resource for trait improvement. Nat Biotechnol 31, 240–246 (2013)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1306">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Young, N. D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520–524 (2011)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1309">22</a></sup>. Among the LTR retrotransposons, Gypsy and Copia constituted 19% and 10% of the adzuki bean genome sequences, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S14</a>). DNA transposons, CACTA, Mutator, PIF-Harbinger, hAT, Helitron, MULE-MuDR and Tc1-Mariner, were also detected, comprising approximately 2.7% of the adzuki bean genome sequences.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Domestication traces of adzuki bean</h3><p>Human selection activities on crops have inflicted major effects on crop genomes, which ultimately result in domestication syndrome, which is marked by loss of seed shattering, minimization of seed dormancy and an increase in both seed size and number<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Harris, D. R. Domestication of plants in the old world: The origin and spread of cultivated plants in west Asia, Europe and the Nile valley. Agr Hist Rev 49, 226–227 (2001)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1324">23</a></sup>. To identify the domestication traces within the genome of <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i>, we sequenced wild relatives of adzuki bean, <i>V. nepalensis</i> (AusTRCF85148) and <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>nipponensis</i> (IT241912) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S2</a>). Single paired-end libraries from each genotype were sequenced using Illumina HiSeq 2000 generating about 50 Gb of short reads which represents more than 85-fold coverage of the adzuki bean genome. We also analyzed short read sequences of wild adzuki bean, <i>Vigna nakashimae</i> (IT178530), which was generated in our previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Lestari, P. et al. Genome-wide single nucleotide polymorphism discovery and validation in adzuki bean. Mol Breeding 33, 497–501 (2014)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1350">24</a></sup>.</p><p>The 22-base k-mer frequency analysis of these short read sequences revealed a variation of genome size among wild adzuki beans (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S1</a>). This is especially true for <i>V. nakashimae</i>, which showed a much larger genome size than <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> and the rest of the wild adzuki beans.</p><p>We mapped the short read sequences onto our adzuki bean reference genome using Burrows-Wheeler Aligner software (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S15 and S16</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1375">25</a></sup>. <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>nipponensis</i> had 667,097 SNPs compared with the reference genome and the SNP frequency to total mapped regions (SNPs per kb) was 1.82. 75,476 of the identified SNPs are located within coding regions and 3,840 of those SNPs could potentially cause non-synonymous protein sequence changes in 1,421 gene products. We also identified 97, 932 insertions and deletions (INDELs) of which 14,033 were in coding regions.</p><p><i>V. nepalensis</i> showed much higher polymorphism than <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>nipponensis</i> as demonstrated by the presence of 3,511,378 SNPs (10.24 SNPs per kb). The number of SNPs in coding region was 433,210 and there were 18,034 non-synonymous SNPs that affected 6,464 genes. A total of 410,232 INDELs were identified and 71,337 were within coding regions.</p><p><i>V. nakashimae</i> showed 3,342,795 SNPs and 624,856 intragenic SNPs. However, the ratio of aligned reads to total reads and the predicted number of INDELs were notably lower than those of other wild adzuki beans. This could be due to the difficulty of read mapping between the reference species (<i>V. angularis</i>) and the highly diverged species (<i>V. nakashimae</i>) showing notable genome size discrepancy (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S1</a>).</p><p>We compared orthologs between cultivated and wild adzuki bean to elucidate domestication-related loci. The orthologous coding sequences of <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>nipponensis</i> and <i>V. nepalensis</i> were reconstructed by substituting the coding sequences of <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> with their respective SNP data. Since <i>V. nakashimae</i> short reads were not efficiently mapped onto our reference genome, we performed <i>de novo</i> assembly of the short read sequences using ABySS software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Simpson, J. T. et al. ABySS: a parallel assembler for short read sequence data. Genome Res 19, 1117–1123 (2009)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1436">26</a></sup> and implemented gene prediction (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S4</a>). From the ortholog comparison between cultivated and wild adzuki bean, we calculated the ratio of the number of nonsynonymous substitutions per non-synonymous site (Ka) to the number of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (Ks) to estimate the selective pressure on each gene (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>nipponensis</i> showed the lowest number of polymorphic genes (1,823) in the Ka/Ks calculation. We observed one consistent peak at Ka/Ks value of 0.2 suggesting purifying selection between cultivated and three wild adzuki beans (Ka/Ks &lt; 1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Kimura, M. Preponderance of Synonymous Changes as Evidence for Neutral Theory of Molecular Evolution. Nature 267, 275–276 (1977)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1453">27</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). Notably, the Ka/Ks distributions of <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> x <i>V.angularis</i> var. <i>nipponensis</i> and <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> x <i>V. nepalensis</i> were highly similar consistently showing three peaks (0.2, 0.5 and 0.7 ~ 0.8). In both comparisons, a total of 307, 152 and 75 genes were commonly found within the three peaks, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S17</a>). This suggests three different degrees of selection pressure on these loci between cultivated and wild adzuki bean. Even though a Ka/Ks of less than 1 has been interpreted as a signature of purifying selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Kimura, M. Preponderance of Synonymous Changes as Evidence for Neutral Theory of Molecular Evolution. Nature 267, 275–276 (1977)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1485">27</a></sup>, a subset of the genes in each peak can be candidates for explaining the difference between wild and cultivated adzuki bean, such as speciation and domestication (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S17</a>). For example, the homologs to disease related genes in the second (0.4 ~ 0.6) and third peak (0.6 ~ 0.9) such as Vang03g15160, Vang02g14420, Vang0291s00070, Vang0229s00140, Vang02g14420 may possess novel disease resistance alleles in wild adzuki beans which are distinct to those in cultivated adzuki bean (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S17</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep08069/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep08069/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep08069_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep08069/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep08069_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="347" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Selection pressure for each locus in adzuki bean genome between cultivated (<i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i>) and wild adzuki beans (<i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>nipponenesis</i>, <i>V. nepalensis</i> and <i>V. nakashimae</i>).</p><p>(A) Circos map displaying Ka/Ks value of each locus (from outer most layer), the positions of common loci in purifying selection and gene density. The most inner ribbons indicate the duplicated synteny blocks (B) Density plot showing the distribution of Ka/Ka values between cultivated and wild adzuki beans.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep08069/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Marker development and its utilization in breeding program</h3><p>In order to produce genetic markers that are easily applicable for QTL mapping and marker assisted breeding programs, we identified simple sequence repeat (SSR) markers using MISA software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Thiel, T., Michalek, W., Varshney, R. K. &amp; Graner, A. Exploiting EST databases for the development and characterization of gene-derived SSR-markers in barley (Hordeum vulgare L.). Theor Appl Genet 106, 411–422 (2003)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1543">28</a></sup>. A total of 143,113 SSRs were detected and the number of tri-repeat unit SSRs, the preferred type for genotyping, were 1,941 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S18</a>).</p><p>We predicted the associated QTLs to these SSR markers using translational genomics approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Cannon, S. B., May, G. D. &amp; Jackson, S. A. Three Sequenced Legume Genomes and Many Crop Species: Rich Opportunities for Translational Genomics. Plant Physiol 151, 970–977 (2009)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1553">29</a></sup>. <i>G. max</i> is a warm-season model legume crop closely related to <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> showing co-linearity of most of the gene content (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). Hence, the predicted QTLs from the comparison between <i>G. max</i> and <i>V. angularis</i> would be useful clue to determine genomic regions related to agriculturally important traits in <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Grant, D., Nelson, R. T., Cannon, S. B. &amp; Shoemaker, R. C. SoyBase, the USDA-ARS soybean genetics and genomics database. Nucleic Acids Res 38, 843–846 (2010)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1582">30</a></sup>. We translated the genomic positions of 2,010 QTL-associated SSR markers of <i>G. max</i> to corresponding genomic positions of <i>V. angularis</i> by 569 orthologous synteny blocks (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S19</a>) and plotted the agriculturally important QTLs such as flowering time, maturity, seed size, yield and disease resistance onto a circular map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). The disease resistance QTLs were likely to be around the 87 genes that code for nucleotide-binding site (NBS) and leucine-rich repeat (LRR) domains, which are commonly associated with disease resistance (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S20</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Meyers, B. C., Kozik, A., Griego, A., Kuang, H. H. &amp; Michelmore, R. W. Genome-wide analysis of NBS-LRR-encoding genes in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 15, 1683–1683 (2003)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1605">31</a></sup>. The flanking markers of these translated QTLs can be used for breeding programs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S19</a>). We also constructed a database containing the gene information, genetic markers and associated QTL data in Jbrowse environment, which can be accessed at <a href="http://plantgenomics.snu.ac.kr/">http://plantgenomics.snu.ac.kr/</a> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome evolution of adzuki bean</h3><p>Using 60 orthologs of <i>P. vulgaris</i>, <i>V. radiata</i>, <i>V. nakashimae</i>, <i>V. nepalensis</i>, <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>nipponensis</i> and <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i>, we constructed a species tree (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S21</a>). <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> formed a distinct clade that included the wild adzuki beans in the species tree. As expected from genome size difference, <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>nipponensis</i> was closest to <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i>, whereas <i>V. nakashimae</i> was more diverged. We estimated the speciation times, which were calibrated using the divergence time, 8.0 million years ago (MYA), between <i>Phaseolus</i> and <i>Vigna</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Lavin, M., Herendeen, P. &amp; Wojciechowski, M. Evolutionary Rates Analysis of Leguminosae Implicates a Rapid Diversification of Lineages during the Tertiary. Syst Biol 54, 575–594 (2005)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1691">32</a></sup>. For our sampled accessions, the minimum speciation time between cultivated and wild adzuki bean was 0.05 MYA, which predated the archaeological evidence for adzuki bean cultivation (~5,000 years before present)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lee, G.-A. Archaeological perspectives on the origins of azuki (Vigna angularis). Holocene 23, 453–459 (2013)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1695">4</a></sup>. The Ks density plot calculated using synteny relationship within the legume genomes revealed the single ancient whole genome duplication at ~53.3 MYA based on the substitution rate, 6.1 × 10<sup>−9</sup> <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Lynch, M. &amp; Conery, J. S. The evolutionary fate and consequences of duplicate genes. Science 290, 1151–1155 (2000)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1701">33</a></sup>, which is commonly shared among <i>V. angularis</i>, <i>V. radiata</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). We identified 1,273 tandemly duplicated genes in the adzuki bean genome; these genes are highly enriched in the gene ontology categories of defense response, oxidation reduction and phosphorylation, which is consistent with findings in other plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S22</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S9</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Rizzon, C., Ponger, L. &amp; Gaut, B. S. Striking similarities in the genomic distribution of tandemly arrayed genes in Arabidopsis and rice. Plos Comput Biol 2, 989–1000 (2006)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1725">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Hanada, K., Zou, C., Lehti-Shiu, M. D., Shinozaki, K. &amp; Shiu, S. H. Importance of lineage-specific expansion of plant tandem duplicates in the adaptive response to environmental stimuli. Plant Physiol 148, 993–1003 (2008)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1728">35</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep08069/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep08069/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep08069_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep08069/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep08069_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1000" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Analyses of the evolution of adzuki bean with comparison to closely related warm season legumes.</p><p>(A) Estimation of WGD using the density plot of Ks values within each gene set of <i>V. angularis</i> (blue), <i>P. vulgaris</i> (green), <i>V. radiata</i> (purple) and <i>G. max</i> (red). (B) Species tree of cultivated adzuki bean (<i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i>), wild adzuki beans (<i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>nipponensis</i>, <i>V. nepalensis</i> and <i>V. nakashimae</i>), <i>V. radiata</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i>. The divergence times of each nodes were estimated by a Bayesian MCMC method calibrated by the root divergence time (8 MYA) between <i>Phaseolus</i> and <i>Vigna</i>. The posterior probability of each node is depicted in parentheses.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep08069/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>Among Asian <i>Vigna</i>, adzuki bean is an economically important grain legume due to its nutritional properties and popular use in dessert foods. A better understanding of adzuki bean genetics is important for more efficient breeding and in light of an increase in biotic and abiotic stresses on crops that may accompany climate change. The adzuki bean reference genome sequence and re-sequencing efforts of two different wild adzuki beans, <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> and <i>V. nepalensis</i>, presented in this manuscript, are a rich source of genetic markers, loci under degrees of selection pressure and putative candidate genes for several agriculturally important traits that were derived from translational genomics with <i>G. max</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#Fig1">Fig 1a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#Fig3">Fig 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S19</a>).</p><p>Plant genome complexity and the length of repeats that often exceed the insert size of present mate pair library technology can limit the <i>de novo</i> assembly of NGS reads to a certain saturation point. We largely improved the assembly by implementing the synteny-based scaffolding approach using gene order conservation between closely related legume species (<i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i>, <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i>). Thus, this method could be used as an alternative to the longer sequencing reads or mate pair library with larger insert size solving the assembly problem. In addition, we anchored super-scaffolds onto the genetic map constructed using the GBS-based genotypes of F<sub>4</sub> recombinant inbred lines of <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> and <i>V. nakashimae</i>. Due to the segregation distortion, relatively small amounts of GBS-derived SNPs were used for constructing the genetic map. The possible cause of this segregation distortion in this population is the genome size difference of parental lines, which may result in ectopic recombination (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S1</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Jenczewski, E. et al. Insight on segregation distortions in two intraspecific crosses between annual species of Medicago (Leguminosae). Theor Appl Genet 94, 682–691 (1997)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1867">36</a></sup>.</p><p>Even in our limited number of cultivated and wild adzuki bean samples, we could catch a glimpse of the speciation time between <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> and <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>nipponensis</i>, which occurred at around 50,000 years ago. As the domestication time evidenced by archaeology was ~5,000 before present<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lee, G.-A. Archaeological perspectives on the origins of azuki (Vigna angularis). Holocene 23, 453–459 (2013)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1886">4</a></sup>, we could hypothesize that the domestication event by start of human cultivation occurred after speciation between wild and cultivated adzuki bean. Additional population-level re-sequencing efforts of cultivated and wild adzuki beans would reveal the speciation time and also the domestication sites at higher confidence level and also test the previous hypothesis of multiple domestication sites of <i>V. angularis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lee, G.-A. Archaeological perspectives on the origins of azuki (Vigna angularis). Holocene 23, 453–459 (2013)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1893">4</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Plant materials</h3><p>The cultivated adzuki bean accession, Gyeongwon (<i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i>, IT213134) and wild adzuki bean accession (<i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>nipponenesis</i>, IT241912) were provided by the Rural Development Administration (RDA) Genebank Information Center in Korea. The other wild adzuki bean (<i>V. nepalensis</i>, AusTRCF85148) was sourced from the Australian Collections of Plant Genetic Resources in Australia.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome assembly and gene prediction</h3><p>For genome size estimation, we used Jellyfish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Marcais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764–770 (2011)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1932">10</a></sup> software to observe K mer (22-mer for this study) frequency distribution by “jellyfish count” command with parameter -C, -m 22, -s 50G, -t 10 and -c 6. “jellyfish histo” command created the histogram of K mer frequency and the peak frequency could be observed. The sequencing depth (N) was estimated by the equation: N = M * L/(L − K + 1) where M is peak K mer frequency, L is read length, K is K mer length.</p><p>We also estimated the genome size by the flowcytometry analysis using procedure modified from Arumuganathan and Earle <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E. Estimation of nuclear DNA content of plants by flow cytometry. Plant Mol Biol Rep 9, 229–241 (1991)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1941">37</a></sup>. Briefly, the procedure consists of preparing suspensions of intact nuclei by chopping of 50 mg adzuki bean leaf tissues in MgSO<sub>4</sub> buffer mixed with DNA standards and stained with propidium iodide in a solution containing DNAase-free-RNAase. Fluorescence intensities of the stained nuclei are measured by FACScalibur flow cytometer (Becton-Dickinson, San Jose, CA). Values for nuclear DNA content are estimated by comparing fluorescence intensities of the nuclei of the testing sample with those of an appropriate internal DNA standard such as nuclei from Chicken Red blood cells (2.5 pg/2C), <i>Glycine max</i> (2.45 pg./2C), <i>Oryza sativa</i> cv. Nipponbare (0.96 pg/2C), or <i>Arabidopaia</i> <i>thaliana</i> (0.36 pg/2C). For each measurement, the propidium iodide fluorescence area signals (FL2-A) from 1000 nuclei were collected and analyzed by CellQuest software (Becton-Dickinson, San Jose, CA).</p><p>For genome assembly of cultivated adzuki bean, we used the Illumina HiSeq 2000 using types of sequencing libraries such as two fragment libraries and two 5 kb and one 10 kb mate pair libraries. One additional single linear library was sequenced using the Roche GS-FLX+ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S23</a>). The assembles of Illumina HiSeq 2000 and GS-FLX+ read sequences were implemented using ALLPATHS-LG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A 108, 1513–1518 (2011)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1969">9</a></sup> and Newbler, respectively. ALLPATH-LG was run with default parameter and Newbler was run with option -large and -trim for “runProject” command. The contigs generated by Newbler were chopped into pseudo fragment library reads and supplied to ALLPATHS-LG again. Software ABYSS was used for the assembly of <i>V. nakahsimae</i> using the option k = 84 and q = 20 for “abyss-pe” command. The traces of repetitive sequences were searched using LTR-harvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1976">38</a></sup> and TransposonPSI (<a href="http://transposonpsi.sourceforge.net/">http://transposonpsi.sourceforge.net/</a>) with default parameters. The captured repetitive sequence blocks were annotated by LTR-digest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Steinbiss, S., Willhoeft, U., Gremme, G. &amp; Kurtz, S. Fine-grained annotation and classification of de novo predicted LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res 37, 7002–7013 (2009)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1988">39</a></sup> using a set of hmm signatures: PF03078.8, PF00385.17, PF01393.12, PF04094.7, PF07253.4, PF00552.14, PF05380.6, PF00077.13, PF08284.4, PF00078.20, PF07727.7, PF06815.6, PF06817.7, PF03732.10, PF00075.17, PF01021.12, PF04195.5, PF00692.12, PF00692.12 and PF00098. The hmm signature of AP_ty1copia and AP_ty3gypsy elements was built using their alignment information from GyDB<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Llorens, C. et al. The Gypsy Database (GyDB) of mobile genetic elements: release 2.0. Nucleic Acids Res 39, D70–D74 (2011)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1992">40</a></sup>. We further utilized the <i>G. max</i> TE classification based on sequence homology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Du, J. C. et al. SoyTEdb: a comprehensive database of transposable elements in the soybean genome. BMC Genomics 11, 113 (2010)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d38204e1999">41</a></sup>. The predicted and annotated repetitive sequence blocks were supplied to RepeatMasker software as adzuki bean repetitive sequence library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr Protoc Bioinformatics 25, 4.10.1–4.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d38204e2003">14</a></sup>.</p><p>Adzuki bean gene contents were predicted following MAKER pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: An easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d38204e2010">15</a></sup>. RNA-Seq using Illumina HiSeq 2000 were implemented on mRNAs of the four different tissues (leaf, flower, root and pod) of adzuki bean and <i>de novo</i> assembled using Trinity software with default parameter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat Biotechnol 29, 644–U130 (2011)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d38204e2017">16</a></sup>. Contigs were pooled from <i>de novo</i> assembly and redundant sequences were removed by the CD-HIT-EST algorithm in CD-HIT software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Li, W. Z. &amp; Godzik, A. Cd-hit: a fast program for clustering and comparing large sets of protein or nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 22, 1658–1659 (2006)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d38204e2024">42</a></sup>. The non-redundant transcriptome assemblies were supplied into the MAKER pipeline along with <i>G. max</i> protein sequences and the complete protein sequences of <i>Arabidopsis</i> from Uniprot as evidence for the homology based gene prediction. For <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction, we used AUGUSTUS software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Stanke, M., Tzvetkova, A. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS at EGASP: using EST, protein and genomic alignments for improved gene prediction in the human genome. Genome Biol 7 (Suppl. 1). S11.1–S11.8 (2006)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d38204e2038">43</a></sup> with mungbean (<i>V. radiata</i>) training set. The resulting protein sequences were annotated using InterProScan5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Quevillon, E. et al. InterProScan: protein domains identifier. Nucleic Acids Res 33, W116–W120 (2005)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d38204e2045">44</a></sup>.</p><p>Using the Pfam annotations from InterProScan result, transcription factors (TF) of adzuki bean proteins from <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> and <i>V. nakashimae</i> were classified based on the TF classification rules described in Lang et al<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Lang, D. et al. Genome-Wide Phylogenetic Comparative Analysis of Plant Transcriptional Regulation: A Timeline of Loss, Gain, Expansion and Correlation with Complexity. Genome Biol Evol 2, 488–503 (2010)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d38204e2062">19</a></sup>. For comparative analysis of TF contents, we further classified the TF from the protein sequences of 8 plant genomes (<i>A. thaliana, G. max, M. truncatula, C. cajan, C. arietinum, B. distachyon, Z. mays, O. sativa</i>).</p><p>Using Pfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Finn, R. D. et al. Pfam: the protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res 42, D222–230 (2014)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d38204e2072">45</a></sup> HMM profile of LRR domain (PF00560, PF07725, PF12799, PF13504, PF13516, PF13855 and PF14580) and NB-ARC domain (PF00931), we performed a genome-wide scan to find NBS and LRR domain containing proteins. The sequence blocks matched as LRR and NBS domain were re-aligned to construct <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> specific hmm profiles of LRR and NBS using the HMMER software package with default parameter for “hmmbuild” command<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Finn, R. D., Clements, J. &amp; Eddy, S. R. HMMER web server: interactive sequence similarity searching. Nucleic Acids Res 39, W29–37 (2011)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d38204e2082">46</a></sup>. Using the <i>V. angularis</i>-specific hmm profiles, proteins with NBS and/or LRR domain were identified using HMMER software package with default parameter for “hmmscan” command. The putative functions of NBS-LRR were annotated using the blast result against Uniprot database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Consortium, U. Reorganizing the protein space at the Universal Protein Resource (UniProt). Nucleic Acids Res 40, D71–D75 (2012)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d38204e2090">47</a></sup>.</p><p>We further linked the scaffolds based on the gene order conservation (synteny) of <i>P. vulgaris</i>, <i>V. radiata</i> and <i>V. angularis</i>. The pairwise synteny relationships and the Ks values of gene pairs were calculated using MCSCANX software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, Y. P. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d38204e2106">11</a></sup>. The synteny blocks between <i>V. radiata</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i> were used as links for scaffolding. We retrieved every link that could be deduced from synteny blocks and concatenated each other to build consensus links (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S3</a>). The orientation of the scaffold was determined by the orientation of the gene orders of the synteny blocks used for links.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genetic map construction</h3><p>To construct a genetic map of adzuki bean, we genotyped 133 F<sub>4</sub> lines derived from the cross between Gyeongwon and <i>V. nakashimae</i> (IT178530) using genotyping by sequencing (GBS)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Elshire, R. J. et al. A robust, simple genotyping-by-sequencing (GBS) approach for high diversity species. Plos One 6, e19379 (2011)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d38204e2133">12</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S23</a>). The genomic DNA of each line was extracted and fragmented by the ApeKI restriction enzyme. Validated PCR fragments using the Agilent Technologies Bioanalyzer 2100 were constructed into GBS sequence library and were sequenced using the Illumina HiSeq 2000. We retrieved the sequences flanked with ApeKI restriction site from <i>de novo</i> assembled scaffolds. The short reads were mapped onto the ApeKI flanking sequences using software Bowtie2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat Methods 9, 357–359 (2012)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d38204e2144">13</a></sup>. The genotypes of 133 RILs were collected from the genomic positions showing over 10 read depth and we determined homo- and heterozygous genotypes following SAMtools genotyping statistics with default parameter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d38204e2148">48</a></sup>. If the arrays of paternal or maternal genotypes of 133 individuals within 1 Kb are consistent each other we regarded these sites as co-segregating block and we used one representative site with lowest ‘N’ to construct genetic map. The genotypes of 133 F<sub>4</sub> lines were supplied to JoinMap 4 and we constructed 11 linkage groups using regression mapping algorithm with Kosambi mapping function.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Resequencing of three wild Vigna species</h3><p>The sequence reads of <i>V. nakashimae</i>, <i>V. nepalensis</i> and <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>nipponensis</i> were aligned to our reference genome of <i>V. angularis</i> var. <i>angularis</i> using the BWA-MEM algorithm of BWA software with default options<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d38204e2181">25</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep08069#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S23</a>). The resulting read mapping files were supplied to AddOrReplaceReadGroups, MarkDuplicates, FixMateInformation, RealignerTargetCreator, IndelRealigner, BaseRecalibrator, PrintReads modules included in Picard (<a href="http://picard.sourceforge.net">http://picard.sourceforge.net</a>) and GenomeAnalysisTK (3.1–1 version) to optimize the sequence alignment<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/srep08069#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d38204e2195">49</a></sup>. As there is no comprehensive set of high-confidence known variant site for adzuki bean genome, we used the parameter –run_without_dbsnp_potentially_ruining_quality for BaseRecalibrator step.
